Binding Nature of Previous Laws (Shara'i' Man Qablana)
Discusses the binding authority of laws from earlier prophets

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the binding nature of laws revealed before Prophet Muhammad, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Previous divine laws are not automatically binding on Muslims; they are binding only if adopted by the Sharia of Islam.

This passage addresses a classic usul dispute: Are the laws of previous prophets (shara'i' man qablana) binding on the Muslim community after the advent of Prophet Muhammad? The Shafi'i school generally holds that they remain binding unless abrogated, while the Hanafi position (as preferred) holds that they are not automatically binding. The disagreement hinges on the interpretation of Quran 6:90 and the principle of whether the pleasing nature of those laws entails their permanence. This dispute has practical implications for rulings that are not explicitly mentioned in the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Are previous divine laws binding on Muslims after the Prophet's mission?
2
State the Shafi'i position
They are binding absolutely unless abrogated, based on Quran 6:90 and the principle of continuity.
This is the view of 'general of Shafi'is' and some Hanafi teachers.
3
State the Hanafi position
They are not automatically binding; only binding if mentioned in Quran/Sunnah and not abrogated. The verse commands following guidance, not specific laws.
This is the relied-upon Hanafi view.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Being pleasing to Allah in their time does not guarantee permanence; divine laws can be time-bound.
The text argues that the pleasing nature is contingent on the lifespan of that prophet or a specific period.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Example: stoning punishment for adultery (if found in previous law but not explicitly in Quran) — Shafi'i might consider it, Hanafi would not unless proven from Quran/Sunnah.

Source Text

(القمر) إلى غيرهم وهكذا قال عامة العلماء ومشى عليه الإمام السهر وردي واعتمده الإمام الرازي وابن الصلاح من الشافعية، كذا في "الصبح الصادق" فليس للولي أن يدعو غيره إلى إلهامه ولا أن يمنع مجتهدا يعمل باجتهاده الصحيح وإن علم بإلهام أن اجتهاده خطأ . (القمر) ملحقة بالسنة إلخ : بهذا القول أشار الشارح إلى هذا البحث في فصل أصل السنة لا في الإجماع أو القياس أو الكتاب وقوله واختلف فيها فقال بعضهم إلخ قلت: هذا بيان الاختلاف في التعبد بالشرائع السابقة بعد بعث النبي ، وأما قبله فهل كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يتعبد بها أم لا؟ فأبى بعضهم ذالك كأبي الحسن البصري وجماعة من المتكلمين، وأثبته بعضهم مختلفين فيه أيضًا. ثم اختلف هذا البعض في أن رسول الله بشريعةِ أي نبي كان متعبدًا؟ فقيل : بشرع نوح عليها، وقيل: بشرع إبراهيم علئلا، وقيل: غير ذالك كذا في "الغاية". (السنبلي) واختلف فيها : أي في الشرائع السابقة في التعبد بها. (القمر) باقية إلى قيام الساعة لأنها من مرضياته تعالى إلا أن تلزم علينا مطلقا : بناءً على أن كل شريعة تثبت لنبي فهي ! يقوم الدليل على انتساخه، وقد قال الله تعالى: أُولَئِكَ الَّذِينَ هَدَى اللهُ فَيهْدَاهُمُ اقْتَدِهُ (الأنعام: ۹۰) فعلى هذا يلزمنا شرائع من قبلنا مطلقا، وعليه عامة أصحاب الشافعي وبعض مشائخنا ولقائل أن يقول: إن كونها من مرضياته تعالى لا يستلزم أن يبقى إلى الساعة، لِمَ لا يجوز أن تكون من مرضياته تعالى إلى حياة ذالك النبي أو إلى مدة معينة، فإنه تعالى حكيم يضل بمصالح ، ولا يُسأل مما يفعل. (القمر)
صد الله
سب
إيراد
Chapter on the actions of the Prophet. The Prophet (peace be upon him) and other saints: For him it is a decisive proof, but for others it is not with this quality. His inspiration is a division of revelation that is a proof transmitted to the general creation. The inspiration of saints is a proof for themselves, if it agrees with the Sharia, and it does not extend to others unless we take their saying by way of etiquette. Then he began a discussion of [the laws] before us, from the perspective that they are annexed to the Sunnah, and there is disagreement about them. Some said: They are binding upon us absolutely. 'Laws of' meaning from Allah through the previous prophets. 'Decisive proof' etc., meaning that inspiration is a decisive proof for him, i.e., his inspiration is a decisive evidence that it is not permissible to oppose. As for inspiration in the right of others, i.e., the inspiration of other saints, it is not with this quality, i.e., it is not a decisive proof, rather it is speculative due to the lack of infallibility, so we are not obliged to follow it; rather it is permissible to oppose it. (Al-Qamar): 'So his inspiration etc.' Apparently the 'fa' is for explanation or causation, and in any case there is no correspondence between the exposition of the commentator and the conclusion of the text: the inspiration of the saint according to the commentator's exposition is a proof for himself, not for others, but the conclusion of the text is that the inspiration of the saint is not a proof at all, neither for himself nor for others, as is apparent from the wording of the text. This is the choice of Ibn al-Humam. It may be argued that inspiration is nothing but an impression in the heart, and this is from fantasies, so it is not regarded. But this argument is weak: the inspiration of the saint is not like our random thoughts; rather his inspiration is that a matter falls into his heart from his necessary certain knowledge that it is from Allah, so it is a proof without doubt. Such has been said. (Al-Qamar): 'it is a proof' means a decisive proof without hesitation. (Al-Qamar): 'if it agrees with the Sharia etc.' There is an indication: if the inspiration of the saint contradicts the Muhammadan Sharia, then it is not a proof, neither for himself nor for others; it is only from the misguiding devil. (Al-Qamar): 'to others' and thus said the generality of scholars, and Imam al-Suhrawardi followed it, and Imam al-Razi and Ibn al-Salah from the Shafi'is relied on it. Such is in 'al-Subh al-Sadiq'. So the saint may not call others to his inspiration, nor prevent a mujtahid from acting on his correct ijtihad, even if he knows through inspiration that his ijtihad is wrong. (Al-Qamar): 'appended to the Sunnah etc.': By this statement the commentator pointed to this research in the chapter on the foundation of the Sunnah, not in consensus or analogy or the Book. And his saying 'and they differed about it' etc. I say: This is an explanation of the disagreement regarding acting upon the previous laws after the sending of the Prophet. As for before it, was the Messenger of Allah acting upon them or not? Some denied it, like Abu al-Hasan al-Basri and a group of theologians, and some affirmed it, with further disagreement among them. Then these disagreed about which prophet's law the Messenger acted upon. It was said: upon the law of Nuh (peace be upon him), and said: upon the law of Ibrahim, and said: other than that, such is in 'al-Ghayah'. (Al-Sunbulī): 'and they differed about it' i.e., about the previous laws regarding acting upon them. (Al-Qamar): 'remaining until the Day of Resurrection because they are from His pleasing acts unless they are binding upon us absolutely': based on the idea that every law established for a prophet, unless evidence of its abrogation exists, and Allah said: 'Those are the ones whom Allah has guided, so follow their guidance' (al-An'am 6:90). Upon this, the laws of those before us are binding upon us absolutely. And upon this are the generality of the Shafi'is and some of our teachers. But it can be said: That they are from His pleasing acts does not necessitate that they remain until the Hour. Why is it not permissible that they be from His pleasing acts until the life of that prophet or for a specified period? Verily, He is Wise, He does as He wills, and He is not asked about what He does. (Al-Qamar).
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This passage covers two important usul topics: the authority of inspiration (ilham) and the binding nature of previous divine laws. First, it distinguishes between the Prophet's ilham and that of saints (awliya). For the Prophet, ilham is a form of revelation that is decisive proof (hujja qati'a) binding on all people. For saints, their ilham is not proof for others, because saints are not infallible (ma'sum). There is a disagreement among Hanafi scholars whether the saint's ilham is proof for himself: some say it is (as per the commentator), while others (like Ibn al-Humam) say it is not proof at all. However, if the saint's ilham contradicts the Sharia, it is undoubtedly from Satan. This is important for the principle that no one can impose their personal spiritual experiences on others, and a mujtahid is not to be prevented from his valid ijtihad even if a saint claims inspiration to the contrary. Second, the passage discusses the laws of previous prophets (shara'i' man qablana) and whether they are binding on the Muslim community. The text presents the view of the majority of Shafi'is and some Hanafi scholars that they are binding absolutely, based on the Quranic verse commanding to follow the guidance of previous prophets (6:90). Others argue that they are only binding if not abrogated, and that being pleasing to Allah in their time does not guarantee their permanence. The commentator notes that this discussion belongs under the sources of law as an annex to the Sunnah, not to consensus or analogy.

Key Terms
شرائع من قبلنا (shara'i' man qablana): The divine laws revealed to prophets before Prophet Muhammad, such as those of Noah, Abraham, Moses, Jesus, etc.
حجة قاطعة (hujja qati'a): A conclusive proof that is certain and must be followed; cannot be opposed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position on the binding nature of previous divine laws (shara'i' man qablana)?
A) They are binding on Muslims absolutely unless abrogated.
B) They are not automatically binding; they are binding only if mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah.
C) They are binding only for the people of that time, not for Muslims.
D) They are binding only if they agree with the Hanafi school.

Key Takeaway
Previous divine laws are not automatically binding on Muslims; only those confirmed in the Quran and Sunnah are part of the Sharia.

The Legal Authority of Divine Inspiration (Ilham) in Hanafi Usul
Explains ilham as a type of wahy and its authority for prophets and saints

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi positions on the authority of ilham (divine inspiration) for saints and apply the condition that ilham must agree with the Shariah to be considered a proof.

Core Idea
Ilham of a saint is a personal proof only if it agrees with Sharia; it is not obligatory for others.

In this lesson, we examine a nuanced discussion from Noor al-Anwar about the status of divine inspiration (ilham) in Islamic legal epistemology. The passage distinguishes between the inspiration of prophets, which is definitive and universal, and that of saints, which is speculative and personal. We will explore the disagreement between Ibn al-Humam and the commentator (al-Qamar) on whether ilham is a proof for the saint himself, and the conditions that govern its validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of inspiration
First, distinguish between prophetic inspiration (wahy) and saintly inspiration (ilham). Note that for the Prophet, ilham is a definitive proof (hujja qati'a) for all; for saints, it is not.
The key term 'hujja qati'a' is used only for the Prophet.
2
State the Hanafi positions on saintly ilham
Two views are presented: (1) According to the text's summary (matn) and Ibn al-Humam, ilham is not a proof at all, not even for the saint. (2) According to the commentator (sharh) and many scholars, it is a proof for the saint himself, provided it agrees with Shariah.
The commentator uses 'fa' for interpretation, leading to a discrepancy.
3
Apply the condition of agreement with Shariah
If the ilham contradicts the Shariah, it is from Satan and carries no weight. Even if it agrees, it remains personal and cannot be imposed on others.
This condition is a safeguard against false inspiration.
4
Examine the consequences for ijtihad
A saint who receives ilham cannot prevent a mujtahid from acting on his own correct ijtihad, even if the saint's ilham tells him the mujtahid is wrong. This respects the independence of ijtihad.
This shows that ilham does not override other legal proofs.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that saintly ilham is not a proof that can be used to derive laws for the community. It is at best a personal guidance.
This follows the majority of Hanafi usulis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أفعال النبي بيان أقسام الوحي عليه وغيره من الأولياء؛ فإنه حجة قاطعة في حقه وإن لم يكن في حق غيره بهذه الصفة، فإلهامه قسم من الوحي يكون حجة متعدّية إلى عامة الخلق، وإلهام الأولياء حجة في حق أنفسهم، إن وافق الشريعة و لم يتعدّ إلى غيرهم إلا إذا أخذنا بقولهم بطريق الآداب. (القمر) فإلهامه إلخ الظاهر أن الفاء للتفسير أو للتعليل، وعلى كل تقدير فلا تطابق بين تقرير الشارح ومحصل المتن؛ فإن إلهام الولي على تقرير الشارح حجة في حق نفسه لا في حق غيره، ومحصل المتن أن إلهام الولي ليس حجة أصلاً، لا في حق نفسه ولا في حق غيره كما هو الظن من عبارت المتن، وهذا هو مختار ابن الهمام، وقد يستدل عليه بأن الإلهام ليس إلا الإلقاء في القلب، وهذا من الخيالات، فلا اعتداد به وهذا الاستدلال واه؛ فإن إلهام الولي ليس كخطراتنا بل إلهامه أن يقع في قلبه أمر من علمه الضروري القطعي بأنه من الله فهو حجة بلا ريب كذا قيل. (القمر) يكون حجة أي حجة قطعية بلا امتراء. (القمر) إن وافق الشريعة إلخ فيه إيماء، أي إن إلهام الولي إن خالف الشريعة المحمدية فهو ليس بحجة، لا في حق نفسه ولا في حق غيره، إنما هو من الشيطان الضال المضل. (القمر) إلى غيرهم وهكذا قال عامة العلماء ومشى عليه الإمام السهر وردي واعتمده الإمام الرازي وابن الصلاح من الشافعية، كذا في "الصبح الصادق" فليس للولي أن يدعو غيره إلى إلهامه ولا أن يمنع مجتهدا يعمل باجتهاده الصحيح وإن علم بإلهام أن اجتهاده خطأ.
Section on the actions of the Prophet. Explanation of the types of revelation (wahy) for him and others besides him from the saints; for it is a definitive proof concerning him, even if it is not so concerning others. So his inspiration is a type of revelation that becomes a proof extending to the generality of creation. And the inspiration of the saints is a proof concerning themselves, if it agrees with the Shariah, and it does not extend to others unless we take their statements by way of proper conduct. (Al-Qamar) 'His inspiration, etc.' Apparently the 'fa' is for interpretation or for causation, and on either account there is no correspondence between the commentator's exposition and the summary of the text. For the inspiration of the saint according to the commentator is a proof concerning himself, not concerning others. And the summary of the text is that the inspiration of the saint is not a proof at all, neither concerning himself nor concerning others, as is the obvious meaning from the wording of the text. And this is the choice of Ibn al-Humam. And it is inferred that inspiration is nothing but an impulse in the heart, and this is from fantasies, so no consideration is given to it. And this inference is weak; for the inspiration of the saint is not like our passing thoughts, rather his inspiration is that something occurs in his heart from his necessary definitive knowledge that it is from Allah, so it is a proof without doubt. Thus it is said. (Al-Qamar) 'It is a proof' i.e., a definitive proof without hesitation. (Al-Qamar) 'If it agrees with the Shariah, etc.' In it is an indication, i.e., if the inspiration of the saint contradicts the Muhammadan Shariah, then it is not a proof, neither concerning himself nor concerning others; it is from the misleading devil. (Al-Qamar) 'To others' etc. Thus said the general body of scholars, and upon it walked Imam al-Suhrawardi, and it was relied upon by Imam al-Razi and Ibn al-Salah of the Shafi'is. So in 'Al-Subh al-Sadiq': It is not for the saint to call others to his inspiration, nor to prevent a mujtahid from acting upon his correct ijtihad, even if he knows through inspiration that his ijtihad is wrong.
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This passage deals with the authority of divine inspiration (ilham) as a source of knowledge in Islamic law. The author opens by distinguishing between the inspiration received by the Prophet ﷺ and that received by saints. For the Prophet, ilham is a definitive proof (hujja qati'a) that cannot be opposed, as it is a type of revelation (wahy) protected from error. For saints, however, ilham is not definitive but speculative (zanni), because saints are not infallible. Therefore, following the ilham of a saint is not obligatory; one may oppose it. The commentator (the one who wrote the sharh) notes a disagreement between the wording of the text (matn) and the interpretation of the commentator: the matn suggests that saint's ilham is not a proof at all, even for the saint himself, while the commentator makes it a proof for himself if it aligns with Sharia. The preferred view according to Ibn al-Humam is that it is not a proof at all; the counter-argument is that saintly ilham is a certain knowledge event, not a mere thought. The passage further conditions that if the ilham contradicts the Shariah, it is from Satan and carries no weight. Finally, it states that a saint cannot oblige others to follow his ilham, and cannot prevent a mujtahid from acting on his own correct ijtihad. This is the relied-upon position among the majority of scholars, including notable Shafi'is like al-Razi and Ibn al-Salah.

Key Terms
إلهام (ilham): إلقاء في القلب من علم ضروري بأنه من الله
وحي (wahy): الإعلام في الخفاء، وهو قسمان: وحي نبوة وإلهام
حجة قاطعة (hujja qati'a): دليل قطعي لا يجوز مخالفته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in the text, is it obligatory to follow the ilham of a saint?
A) Yes, absolutely
B) Yes, if it agrees with Shariah
C) No, it is not obligatory; one may oppose it
D) No, it is forbidden to follow it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, saintly inspiration (ilham) is at best a personal guidance, never a binding legal proof for others.

Obligation of Following a Tabi'i Accepted by the Companions
Rule with proof and refutation of objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on taqlid of a Tabi'i and analyze the evidence from the story of Ali and Shuraih to derive the ruling.

Core Idea
A Tabi'i whose fatwa was accepted by the Companions is like a Companion in obligation of taqlid.

In this passage, the author addresses a key question in usul al-fiqh: can a Muslim follow (taqlid) the opinion of a Tabi'i as a binding legal authority? The Hanafi school holds that if a Tabi'i's fatwa became known and was accepted during the time of the Companions, it carries the same weight as a Companion's opinion. The story of Caliph Ali litigating before the Tabi'i judge Shuraih serves as a powerful precedent.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi rule on taqlid of a Tabi'i
Read the opening sentence: 'As for the Tabi'i, if his fatwa appeared in the time of the Companions, like Shuraih, he was like them according to some, and that is the correct view, so his taqlid is obligatory.' Identify the key conditions: the fatwa must have been known and accepted in the Companions' era.
The condition 'appeared in the time of the Companions' is crucial – not all Tabi'un have this status.
2
Identify the primary evidence (dalil)
The author cites the story of Ali litigating before Shuraih regarding his stolen armor. Locate the specific narration in the text. Note that Ali brought witnesses and accepted Shuraih's judgment.
The story is narrated by Ibn Asakir and cited by al-Suyuti.
3
Explain the wajh al-dalalah (how the evidence proves the rule)
Analyze why this story supports the obligation of taqlid. The Companion Ali delegated authority to the Tabi'i judge and accepted his ruling, indicating that the Tabi'i's ijtihad was considered valid and binding in that context.
The text argues that such delegation shows the Tabi'i is 'like them' (the Companions) in authority.
4
Address the objection to the evidence
The passage notes that the author of al-Muslim refuted this inference, arguing that the story only shows opposition occurred, not that it is a general proof. Read the Hanafi response: the delegation itself indicates acceptance, and the general practice of early Muslims supports it.
Some object that Shuraih rejected the testimony of Ali's son, but this does not undermine the overall precedent.
5
Determine practical application: choosing among differing opinions
The passage also discusses how a muqallid should choose when Companions differ: prefer the four caliphs, then Abu Bakr and Umar, then by knowledge. This shows that tarjih is required when possible.
This step connects to the broader topic of taqlid and ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أفعال النبي بيان تقليد التابعي] وأما التابعي فإن ظهرت فتواه في زمن الصحابة كشريح كان مثلهم عند البعض وهو الصحيح، فيجب تقليده، كما روي أن عليا لا تحاكم إلى شريح القاضي في أيام خلافته في درعه، أي التي كانت سُرقت وقال درعي عرفتها مع هذا اليهودي فقال شريح لليهودي: ما تقول؟ قال: درعي وفي يدى فطلب شاهدين من عليه، فأتى علي الله بابنه الحسن وقنبر مولاه ليشهدا عند شريح، أي شريح كان ذلك إلخ : فإنه عُلم حينئذٍ أن كل واحد من القولين ليس بمسموع وإلا فلا يقع تخالف، فكان كل قول من اجتهاد ،قائله فللمقلّد أن يعمل بأيهما شاء، وقيل: إن الصحابة له إذا اختلف فالخلفاء الأربعة أولى، وإن اختلفوا فالشيخان لها ،أولى، وفي باقي الصحابة منه يرجّح بكثرة العلم وغيره من أسباب الترجيح.(القمر) فللمقلّد أن يعمل إلخ : هذا عند تعذر الترجيح، وعند إمكانه يُصار إليه.(القمر) كشريح: عاش مائة وعشرين سنة، واستقضاه عمر على الكوفة، ولم يزل بعد ذلك قاضيًا خمسا له وسبعين سنة، ولم يتعطّل فيها إلا ثلاث سنين امتنع عن القضاء في فتنة ابن الزبير واستعفى شريح الحجاج عن فأعفاه، فلم يقض بين اثنين حتى مات سنة تسع وسبعين كذا نقل ابن الملك. (القمر) القضاء كشريح والحسن وسعيد بن المسيب، والشعبي، والنخعي، ومسروق، وعلقمة . [فتح الغفار ٣٤٩] كان مثله أي في لزوم تقليده؛ لأنه بتسليمهم إياه دخل في جملتهم. (القمر) كان مثله إلخ ولو زاحم بفتوى التابعي رأي الصحابة في عصرهم فليس مثلاً لهم، فلا يكون قوله كالمرفوع لعدم وجود المناط، وهو السماع ومشاهدة القرائن (السنبلي) أن عليا تحاكم إلخ وردّ هذا الاستدلال صاحب "المسلم" وقال: استدلال البعض على صحة تقليد التابعي بردّ شريح شهادة الإمام الحسن منه ومخالفة مسروق ابن عباس لما في إيجاب مائة من الإبل في النذر بذبح الولد أي شاة، فرجع ابن عباس فيما لا يفيد، وقال في الشرح: فإن غاية ما لزم منه أن مخالفة التابعي للصحابي أن مخالفة التابعي للصحابي هي قد وقع، وأما إنها حجة فمن أين؟ نعم، يدل على عدم تقليد التابعي للصحابي الله و ردّ هذه الدلالة أيضًا في الشرح. (السنبلي) رواه ابن عساكر، أورده السيوطي في تاريخ الخلفاء"، وذكره صاحب "الصواعق المحرقة"، وصاحب "السيرة المحمدية"، وعلي القاري شد. [إشراق الأبصار ۲۸] لعلي
Section on the actions of the Prophet: Statement on taqlid of the Tabi'i. As for the Tabi'i, if his fatwa appeared in the time of the Companions, like Shuraih, he was like them according to some, and that is the correct view, so his taqlid is obligatory. As it is narrated that Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) litigated before Shuraih the judge during his caliphate regarding his armor – i.e., which had been stolen – and he said: 'My armor, I recognized it with this Jew.' Shuraih said to the Jew: 'What do you say?' He said: 'My armor, in my hand.' So he requested two witnesses from the plaintiff. Then Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) brought his son Hasan and his freed slave Qanbar to testify before Shuraih – i.e., Shuraih did that etc. Then it was known at that time that each of the two statements is not heard, otherwise there would be no disagreement. So each statement is from the ijtihad of its speaker, and the muqallid may act on whichever he wishes. And it is said: If the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) differed, then the four caliphs are preferable; and if they differed, then the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar) are preferable; and among the remaining Companions, preference is given by abundance of knowledge and other reasons of tarjih. (al-Qamar) So the muqallid may act etc.: This is when tarjih is impossible; when it is possible, it is resorted to. (al-Qamar) Like Shuraih: He lived one hundred and twenty years, and Umar appointed him as judge over Kufa, and he continued as judge for seventy-five years, and he did not stay idle except for three years when he refrained from judging during the fitna of Ibn al-Zubayr, and he asked al-Hajjaj to relieve him, so he relieved him, so he did not judge between two people until he died in the year seventy-nine – so narrated Ibn al-Malik. (al-Qamar) The judgeship: Like Shuraih, al-Hasan, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, al-Sha'bi, al-Nakha'i, Masruq, and 'Alqamah. [Fath al-Ghaffar 349] He was like him – i.e., in the necessity of his taqlid, because by their delegation to him he entered into their group. (al-Qamar) He was like him etc. And if the opinion of the Tabi'i competes with the opinion of the Companions in their time, then he is not like them, so his statement is not like a marfu' due to the lack of the basis, which is hearing and witnessing the circumstantial evidence. (al-Sanbali) That Ali litigated etc. The author of al-Muslim refuted this inference and said: The inference of some on the validity of taqlid of the Tabi'i by Shuraih's rejection of the testimony of Imam Hasan and Masruq's opposition to Ibn Abbas regarding the obligation of one hundred camels in a vow to slaughter a child – i.e., a sheep – so Ibn Abbas retracted on something that does not benefit. And he said in the commentary: The utmost that follows from it is that the Tabi'i's opposition to the Companion occurred, but that it is a proof? No. Rather, it indicates the non-taqlid of the Tabi'i to the Companion (may Allah be pleased with him), and this inference is also rejected in the commentary. (al-Sanbali) Narrated by Ibn Asakir, cited by al-Suyuti in Tarikh al-Khulafa', and mentioned by the author of al-Sawa'iq al-Muhriqa and author of al-Sirah al-Muhammadiyyah, and Ali al-Qari severely. [Ishraq al-Absar 28] For Ali.
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on taqlid of a Tabi'i (a Follower of the Companions). It states that if a Tabi'i's fatwa appeared during the time of the Companions, like Shuraih, then according to the correct view, he is like a Companion in terms of the obligation of following him. The evidence is the story of Caliph Ali litigating before Judge Shuraih regarding his stolen armor. Ali brought his son Hasan and his freed slave Qanbar as witnesses, but Shuraih did not accept the testimony of the son and the slave? The text says 'he rejected the testimony of Imam Hasan' etc. This shows that the Tabi'i's judgment was accepted by Ali, indicating that his fatwa was considered valid and followed. The passage then discusses cases where Companions differ: the muqallid may choose any of their opinions, but if possible, tarjih (preference) is given to the four caliphs, then to Abu Bakr and Umar, then by knowledge. The commentary adds biographical notes on Shuraih: he lived long, was appointed by Umar, and served as judge for 75 years. The passage also addresses an objection: some argue that a Tabi'i's fatwa is not like that of a Companion because he did not witness revelation. But the reply is that if he was delegated by the Companions and his fatwa was accepted, he is effectively part of their group. The author of al-Muslim refuted the inference from the Shuraih story, arguing that it only shows opposition occurred, not that it is a proof for taqlid. The passage ends with a citation of the story's sources.

Key Terms
تابعي (tabi'i): A person who met the Companions and learned from them, but did not meet the Prophet.
شريح (shuraih): A famous Tabi'i judge, known for his long tenure as Qadi of Kufa.
ترجيح (tarjih): Preferring one opinion over another based on evidence or qualifications.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the correct view regarding taqlid of a Tabi'i whose fatwa appeared in the time of the Companions?
A) It is not obligatory to follow him
B) It is obligatory, as he is like a Companion in this regard
C) It is obligatory only if he was a judge
D) It is prohibited to follow him

Key Takeaway
A Tabi'i whose fatwa was accepted in the Companion era is considered a binding authority, as demonstrated by the precedent of Ali and Shuraih.
